4. According to AverroÃ«s, â€œthe first part of the conditional syllogism, which is the cause of something resulting, is called the conditional term (al-muqaddim); the second part, which brings about the result, is called the conditioned term (al-tÄliy).â€ Cf. Short Commentary on Aristotle's Prior Analytics M. 24a17 and margin; P. 26a12â€“13. The first, or conditional, term of a conditional syllogism would be â€œif the sun has risen,

it is daylight.â€ The second, or conditioned, term is what restricts the conditional term and affirms or denies one part of it, e.g., â€œbut the sun has risen.â€ The conclusion of the syllogism is: â€œtherefore, it is daylight.â€ Cf. also Goichon, op. cit., para. 573. AverroÃ«s sometimes used mugaddim to speak of the first half of the condition (â€œif the sun has risenâ€), while using the tÄlin to speak of the second half (â€œit is daylightâ€).

5. AverroÃ«s was apparently thinking of the following kind of inaccurate conclusion: â€œMan exists because animals exist.â€ The first, or conditional, term of this syllogism is: â€œif man exists.â€ If the second, or conditioned, term (â€œthen animals existâ€) is selected and the conditional term brought forth as a conclusion, the syllogism is not accurate, e.g., â€œbut animals exist, therefore, man exists.â€ The reason the syllogism must be inaccurate is that the conditioned term has a wider scope than the conditional term. Similarly, if the opposite of the conditional term is selected and the opposite of the conditioned term brought forth as a conclusion, the resulting syllogism is not accurate, e.g., â€œbut man does not exist; therefore, animals do not exist.â€ The inaccuracy of this syllogism is due to the same reason as in the first example: the conditioned term has a wider scope than the conditional term. The same problem occurs in a slightly different manner in the next paragraph.

There is an exchange in the Protagoras where Socrates said something similar to what is reported here (cf. 331câ€“e), but the reference is much more suggestive of the way in which the doctrine of Protagoras is refuted in the Theaetetus. Even though Protagoras was dead when the conversation took place, Socrates resurrected him, so to speak, by addressing questions to Theodorus as though he were Protagorasâ€”questions to which Theodorus replied without insisting on his own identity. PARAGRAPH (40)

1. According to the strictest teaching of the Metaphysics, there can be no accidental certainty (cf. supra, para. 39, n. 5). Nor did Aristotle ever speak of accidental certainty in Sense and Sensible Objects. To the contrary, he attacked his predecessors and contemporaries in that work for having confused accidental and essential causes; certainty could be attained only about essential causes (cf. Sense and Sensible Objects 437a18â€“438b16, 441a4â€“442b27, and 445a16â€“445b3).

The real issue, then, must be belief which is so strong as to be like certainty and which is due to accidental causes: how does it come about? There is a discussion of accidental causes in On Prophecy in Sleep (462b27â€“464b19), and it results in casting extensive doubt on the phenomenon of prophecy. Aristotle thought dreams were more

often to be explained as coincidences than as signs or as causes of something. Since AverroÃ«s considered the treatise On Prophecy in Sleep to be part of the book KitÄb al-Hiss wa al-MahsÅ«s (Sense and Sensible Objects), he may have been referring to that argument. When he later commented on that collection, he paid careful attention to the question of prophecy in dreams, denying that there was any basic mystery about it. He attributed the phenomenon to the kind of knowledge of causes that arises from a highly developed imaginative faculty. Cf. AverroÃ«s TalkhÄ«s KitÄb al-Hiss wa al-MahsÅ«s li AristÅ« in AristÅ«tÄlis fÄ« al-Nafs, ed. by 'Abd al-RahmÄn BadawÄ« (Cairo: Maktabat al-Nahdah al-Miá¹£riyah, 1954), pp. 224â€“226.

If Alonso and GÃ¤tje are correct in their dating, the commentary on Sense and Sensible Objects was written eleven years after these commentaries (554/1159 versus 565/1170). Consequently, AverroÃ«s's reference here would be to Aristotle's work, not his own. Alonso, GÃ¤tje, and Wolfson also argue that despite the title of TalkhÄ«s (Middle Commentary), AverroÃ«s's book on Sense and Sensible Objects is a Short Commentary (JawÄmi'). Cf. Alonso, op. cit., pp. 55â€“82 and the corroborations from the secondary literature cited by him; GÃ¤tje, Die Epitome der Parva Naturalia des Averroes, op. cit., p. v, n. 2 and pp. xâ€“xi, and Wolfson, "Revised Plan for the Publication of a Corpus Commentariorum Averrois in Aristotelem," op. cit., pp. 90â€“94.

PARAGRAPH (42)

1. According to different traditions, the Prophet claimed that God would never let the nation of Islam agree about something that could lead them astray. It is in this sense that the Muslims are infallible. However, there is a problem about how to treat a member of the community who disagrees with the consensus which the others have supposedly reached. The problem arises from the difficulties of exactly identifying the consensus of the community on any given issue, particularly on theoretical issues. AverroÃ«s discussed this problem more fully in the Decisive Treatise; cf. op. cit., 8:15â€“10:18.

One tradition quotes the Prophet as saying: "Verily, God would not let my nation agree about an error." Another tradition reports a variant of that statement: "Verily, my nation would not agree about an error." In a different tradition, he is reported to have commanded Muslims: "Do not come to agreement about an error." Yet another version quotes the Prophet as praying: "Do not let them agree about an error." Cf. A.J. Wensinck et al., Concordance et Indices de la Tradition Musulmane (7 Vols.; Leiden: E.J. Brill, 1936â€“1969), Vol. I, pp. 97, 364, and 366; Vol. III, p. 517.

2. AbÅ« á¸¤Ämid Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-á¹¬Å«sÄ« al-GhazÄlÄ« (450/1058â€“505/1111) was born at á¹¬us, a small town in KhurÄsÄn near the modern city of Meshed, Iran, and, after living in many other parts of the Middle East, returned there at the end of his life. He was a student of AbÅ« al-Ma'ÄlÄ« for many years and taught theology in Baghdad and later in Nishapur. The best source for a biographical and intellectual account of al-GhazÄlÄ« is his own al-Munqidh Min al-DalÄl, a book which is translated into English as The Deliverance from Error. Of al-GhazÄlÄ«'s intellectual activities, the most important are his attacks on philosophy and his attempt to reform or renew religious belief and practice. The attack on philosophy was brilliantly answered by AverroÃ«s: to al-GhazÄlÄ«'s TahÄfut al-FalÄsifah (The Incoherence of the Philosophers), AverroÃ«s replied with the TahÄfut al-TahÄfut (Incoherence of the Incoherence).

3. This work was written between 493/1099 and 499/1106 and provided a defense of al-GhazÄlÄ«'s views. He examined the question of interpretation and the extent to which tradition and consensus could be used as a basis for knowledge about religious matters. The central theme in the book is indicated in the title: he wished to determine how atheism could be clearly defined.

The difficulties of identifying the precise date when the book was finished are presented by Father Bouyges. He did think, however, that the book was written after The Balance (cf. infra, para. 43, n. 2) and before the Deliverance from Error; cf. Maurice Bouyges, Essai de Chronologie des Oeuvres de al-Ghazali, edited by Michel Allard (Beirut: Imprimerie Catholique, 1959), pp. 50â€“51, 57â€“58, 70â€“71 and 4â€“6.

4. The quote is not exact, but AverroÃ«s caught the spirit of al-GhazÄlÄ«'s thought. According to al-GhazÄlÄ«, one is not called a heretic for holding different opinions about the "branches" or side issues of Islam, except under special circumstances. The only clear case for deciding that someone is a heretic is his denial of the three roots of Islam, i.e., belief in Allah, his Messenger, and the Last Dayâ€”beliefs that AverroÃ«s accepted as crucial in the Decisive Treatise, cf. op. cit., 14:13â€“15:8.

All other questions lead to the charge of heresy only under certain conditions, such as denying the religion passed on by Muhammad or harming the belief of the common people. Consensus is a very obscure matter that al-GhazÄlÄ« preferred to leave for skilled jurists to settle; he even argued that the palpable error of AbÅ« Bakr al-FÄrisÄ« about consensus did not warrant the charge of heresy. Cf. Faysal al-TafrÄ«qah in al-QusÅ«r al-'AwÄlÄ« Min RasÄ'il al-ImÄm al-GhazÄlÄ« (Cairo: Maktabat

al-JundÄ«, no date), pp. 165:14â€“17; 166:5, 14â€“17; 166:18â€“167:6; 169:16â€“170:11.

The Munich manuscript has a sentence explaining this citation, but it has been bracketed as though if were not part of the text: "That is because the dialectical theologians disagree about the conditions to be set down about consensus." (Reading "ijmÄ" [consensus] for "iqnÄ" [persuasion]).

PARAGRAPH (43)

1. The text reads husn al-zann, i.e., literally, "good supposition."

2. This book was written between 493/1099 and 499/1106 but, at any rate, prior to The Distinction between Islam and Atheism; cf. supra, para. 42, note 2 and cf. also M. Bouyges, op. cit., pp. 50â€“58, 70â€“71, and 4â€“6. The Balance is the last of five treatises written by al-GhazÄlÄ« against esoteric doctrines.

3. There is no remark in the book which corresponds to this quotation. Moreover, in this treatise, al-GhazÄlÄ« never used the term al-jumhÅ«r to refer to the common people; instead he used the term al-'awÄm.

AverroÃ«s nevertheless summarized the main idea of the latter part of the book, for al-GhazÄlÄ« did make a distinction between the way the learned grasped religious notions and the way the common people did. The only mention of "miracle" occurred in a context which would make AverroÃ«s's statement appear to be a fair abridgementâ€”but an abridgement, nevertheless. Cf. al-Qiá¹£Äá¹£ al-Mustaá¹£im in al-QusÅ«r al-'AwÄlÄ«, op. cit., pp. 70:9â€“71:2. Cf. also pp. 59:1â€“60:9, 68:6â€“7, 69:11â€“15. Note the long digression on pp. 68:7â€“69:11 in which al-GhazÄlÄ« examined the question of the extent to which dialectic was of any scientific value.

PARAGRAPH (45)

1. Epideictic rhetoric is concerned with praise or blame, is usually addressed to mere spectators, and has honor or disgrace as its end. Cf. Aristotle Rhetoric I. iii. 3â€“5. 1358b4â€“20 and I. ix. 1366a22â€“1368b1; cf. also AverroÃ«s TalkhÄ«á¹£ al-Khaá¹­Äbah, BadawÄ« edition, op. cit., pp. 28â€“31, 71â€“82.

2. Forensic rhetoric is concerned with accusation or defense, is usually addressed to those who judge things that have taken place, and has justice or injustice as its end. Cf. Aristotle Rhetoric I. iii. 3â€“5. 1358b4â€“20 and I. xâ€“xv. 1368b2â€“1377b10; cf. also AverroÃ«s TalkhÄ«á¹£ al-Khaá¹­Äbah, BadawÄ« edition, op. cit., pp. 28â€“31, 83â€“130.

3. Deliberative rhetoric is concerned with exhorting or dissuading, is usually addressed to judges of things to come (like rulers), and has expediency or harm as its end. Cf. Aristotle Rhetoric I. iii. 3â€“5. 1358b4â€“20 and I. ivâ€“viii. 1359a25â€“1366a21; cf. also AverroÃ«s, TalkhÄ«á¹£ al-Khaá¹­Äbah, BadawÄ« edition, op. cit., pp. 28â€“31, 32â€“71.

4. In the Arabic text, this is the apodosis of the sentence beginning with the words: "When Aristotle became aware..."

NOTES TO THE TRANSLATION OF THE SHORT
COMMENTARY ON ARISTOTLE'S POETICS

INVOCATION

1. In addition to this phrase, the Munich manuscript has the following phrase: "Praise be to God, Lord of both worlds." However, the Paris manuscript has the phrase, "I have recourse to Him and place my trust in Him," in addition to the phrase translated in the text.

TITLE

1. Although the word aqÄwil has often been translated as "arguments" (cf. supra, Short Commentary on Aristotle's Topics, para. 1, n. 3), it seemed more appropriate to translate it as "speeches" in this context.

PARAGRAPH (2)

1. In the Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Poetics, AverroÃ«s gave examples of the particles of simile; cf. Talkhis KitÄb AristÅ«tÄlis fÄ« al-Shi'r, BadawÄ« edition, op. cit., pp. 201â€“202.

2. Substitution (tabdil) is an Arabic grammatical and poetical term. When an author places a word or letter in place of another he is said to employ "substitution." Examples of substitution are: "the habits of the gentlemen and the gentlemen of habits"; "... the first house set down for mankind was at Bekkah," Qur'Än III. 97, where Bekkah is used in place of Mekkah; "... Oh, God, make me wealthy through need of You, but do not impoverish me through belief in sufficient wealth to do without You." Cf. al-TahÄnawÄ«, KitÄb KashshÄf IstilÄhÄt al-FunÅ«n, op. cit., Vol. I, pp. 145â€“146; Vol. II, pp. 978â€“989, 1171â€“1172. Note also the discussion of substitution in AverroÃ«s's Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Rhetoric, BadawÄ« edition, op. cit., pp. 204â€“209. Cf. also AbÅ« 'Abd Allah Muhammad al-KhuwÄrizmÄ«, KitÄb MafÄtih al-'UlÅ«m, ed. G. Van Vloten (Leiden: E. J. Brill, 1895), p. 73.

In the Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Poetics (op. cit., p. 202), AverroÃ«s gave two examples of substitution, one a clause from a Qur'Änic verse and the other the verse of poetry quoted in the next sentence of this text (cf. note 3, infra). The citation from the Qur'Än, occurring in surah xxxxi, verse 6 ("... his wives are their mothers..."),

is part of a proof of how close the Prophet is to the believersâ€”so close that his wives could be their mothers.

It is of some interest that AverroÃ«s considered the substitution used in the art of poetics to be more noble than the simile, just as he considered the syllogism used in dialectic to be more noble than the induction and the enthymeme used in rhetoric to be more noble than the example. Cf. Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Topics, Biblioteca Laurenziana, Florence, Codice Orientale Laurenziano, Ms. CLXXX, 54, fol. 91a14â€“18.

3. This verse was cited by AverroÃ«s in the Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Poetics in exactly the same manner, and the editor of that work presented the complete verse in its correct version: "He is the sea from whichever direction you approach him / for his depth is the good deed, while generosity is his coastline" (huw al-bahr min 'ayy al-nawÄhÄ« ataytah / falujjatuh al-ma'rÅ«f wa al-jÅ«d sÄhiluh). According to the editor, the verse is from a poem by AbÅ« TammÄm, cf. Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Poetics, BadawÄ« edition, op. cit., p. 202 and note 2.

4. In the Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Poetics, AverroÃ«s also referred to Empedocles, identified him as a natural scientist, and explained that he was a poet only insofar as his arguments were set forth in meters. AverroÃ«s then suggested that those who made metered arguments about physical questions deserved to be called dialectical theologians more than they deserved to be called poets. Cf. supra, Short Commentary on Aristotle's Rhetoric, para. 31, note 1 and Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Poetics, BadawÄ« edition, op. cit., p. 204; cf. also Poetics 1447b18, 1457b24, 1461a24 for Aristotle's references to Empedocles. Empedocles (490â€“430 B.C.E.) thought that all the structures in the world arose from combinations of four primary substancesâ€”fire, air, water, and earthâ€”by means of two forces: love and strife. Because he held that these primary substances are never destroyed, but only undergo alterations in their mixture, he denied generation and destruction.

PARAGRAPH (5)

1. The word translated here as "treatises" is the same word which has been translated as "speeches" and as "arguments" (aqÄwil). Because each of the commentaries in this collection has been spoken of in a speech or an argument about a certain subject, AverroÃ«s could conclude by speaking of all which preceded as so many speeches or arguments, i.e., aqÄwil.

EPILOGUE

1. The scribe of the Hebrew translation added a colophon in which he stated: "It is finished and completed, praise be to the Lord of the world. The summary of the Art of Logic was completed, praise be to Him who dwells in a hidden, lofty place, on the third day of the month of Tishri in the year five thousand one hundred and seventeen since the period of creation. It was written for myself, as well as for anyone else who wishes [to read it]â€”Ezra bar Shlomo (may his memory live in the world to come), ben Gratnia of Saragossa (may the name protect them)." The Latin edition adds the following: "Verily, God is on high; it is God who aids and sustains; there is none other than God; praise be to God forever. Amen."

INDEX

Secondly, it is designed to serve as a glossary; for that reason, the Arabic equivalents of the technical terms are given. References are to the paragraphs of the texts presented here.
